data-heading-anchor="1"><section class="html-abstract" id="html-abstract">
            <h2 id="html-abstract-title">Abstract</h2>
            <div class="html-p">Producing uniform <span class="html-italic">Cannabis sativa (Cannabis)</span> for medicinal/recreational flower production through sexual propagation has been problematic, leading to dominance of clonal propagation from “mother plants” in the cannabinoid industry, which also faces significant limitations. <span class="html-italic">Cannabis</span> tissue culture (TC) methods have been developed to overcome these challenges, but the long-term health and maintenance of <span class="html-italic">Cannabis</span> explants in TC have been largely overlooked in previous studies. The current study focused on the development of an efficient and optimized micropropagation protocol covering the entire process, with a specific focus on the health and performance in the multiplication stage. Multiplication media were formulated hormone-free to avoid longer-term vitrification issues, resulting in single-main-shoot cultures rather than multiple-shoot cultures. This instigated the use of stage II explant types different from the standard shoot tips previously used for multiple shoot cultures. Multiplication media were further improved from the basal salt composition via nitrogen and calcium additives. The optimized protocol was used on eight diverse <span class="html-italic">Cannabis</span> cultivars to test its applicability across various genetic backgrounds. Results indicated that the protocol was effective for conservation purposes across all cultivars and achieved good long-term multiplication rates for some but not all. The outcomes of this study mark a significant stride towards an efficient <span class="html-italic">Cannabis</span> TC methodology ready for more comprehensive industrial applications.</div>
    </section></div><div id="html-keywords">
        <div class="html-gwd-group">
                            <div id="html-keywords-title">Keywords:</div>
                                                                    <a href="/search?q=Cannabis+tissue+culture" class="html-keywords-link"><i>Cannabis</i> tissue culture</a>;                    <a href="/search?q=Cannabis+sativa" class="html-keywords-link"><i>Cannabis sativa</i></a>;                    <a href="/search?q=micropropagation" class="html-keywords-link">micropropagation</a>;                    <a href="/search?q=shoot+regeneration" class="html-keywords-link">shoot regeneration</a>;                    <a href="/search?q=vitrification" class="html-keywords-link">vitrification</a>        </div>

            </div><div id="html-graphical">
    <div class="html-gwd-group"></div>
</div><div id="Introduction" data-heading-anchor="1"><section id="sec1-plants-13-02544" type="intro"><h2 data-nested="1">  1. Introduction</h2><div class="html-p">Global <span class="html-italic">Cannabis sativa</span> (<span class="html-italic">Cannabis</span>) markets have grown and matured in recent years as an increasing number of countries have legalized <span class="html-italic">Cannabis</span> for medicinal and/or recreational purposes as well as industrial uses. For instance, <span class="html-italic">Cannabis</span> has been legalized for medicinal purposes in various U.S. states, and CBD from industrial hemp is legal country-wide since the 2018 Farm Bill, while Canada and Thailand legalized it for both medicinal and recreational uses in 2018 and 2023, respectively [<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 1" reference-link="#B1-plants-13-02544">1</button>,<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 2" reference-link="#B2-plants-13-02544">2</button>,<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 3" reference-link="#B3-plants-13-02544">3</button>,<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 4" reference-link="#B4-plants-13-02544">4</button>]. Similarly, Germany ended <span class="html-italic">Cannabis</span> prohibition in 2021, becoming the second G7 nation to do so [<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 5" reference-link="#B5-plants-13-02544">5</button>] after Canada, and since April 2024, a legal framework for recreational use has been implemented. In Australia, cultivation of <span class="html-italic">Cannabis</span> for cannabinoid production has been regulated at the federal level by the Office of Drug Control (ODC) since 2016, while industrial hemp cultivation for seed and fiber (thus excluding CBD and terpene production) has been regulated at the state level since 2008 [<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 6" reference-link="#B6-plants-13-02544">6</button>].</div><div class="html-p">The medicinal <span class="html-italic">Cannabis</span> industry depends on high-quality cannabinoid-rich products derived from female-only cultivation, and trimmed flower buds rather than extracts are the preferred product for end use [<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 7" reference-link="#B7-plants-13-02544">7</button>]. To reliably achieve the required quality, production plants need a high level of uniformity. However, <span class="html-italic">Cannabis</span> cultivars are dioecious and highly heterozygous plants, severely complicating uniform propagation from seed. While feminized seed is available, these are still not true to type, resulting in inconsistent performance and product quality. Consequently, the <span class="html-italic">Cannabis</span> industry largely depends on clonal propagation, predominantly achieved via striking cuttings from mother plants. Even large-scale <span class="html-italic">Cannabis</span> producers still rely on this traditional and effort-intense propagation method, where mother plants, which are maintained in a perpetual vegetative state, typically take up around 15% of the growth space [<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 8" reference-link="#B8-plants-13-02544">8</button>]. In addition to being inefficient, this cultivation method exposes the industry to risks of potential loss of germplasm and production capacity due to disease and pest outbreaks. Exacerbating detrimental effects is the decline in vigor among mother stocks over time due to the accumulation of somatic mutations leading to genetic mosaicism [<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 9" reference-link="#B9-plants-13-02544">9</button>] and telomere shortening, requiring the periodical refreshment of the mother stocks.</div><div class="html-p">To address these production bottlenecks, <span class="html-italic">Cannabis</span> growers are seeking biosecure and space-efficient clonal propagation techniques and germplasm conservation strategies. Micropropagation presents a promising approach compared to the traditional rooted cuttings method for the <span class="html-italic">Cannabis</span> industry [<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 10" reference-link="#B10-plants-13-02544">10</button>], as it has the potential to efficiently generate large quantities of disease-free, genetically uniform plants [<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 11" reference-link="#B11-plants-13-02544">11</button>]. Additionally, it provides pathways for long-term conservation. Micropropagation is a plant tissue culture (TC) technique for multiplying plants rapidly by culturing plant tissues or cells in vitro [<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 12" reference-link="#B12-plants-13-02544">12</button>] and has been successfully industrialized for multiple plant-based industries, including horticulture (e.g., orchids and bananas), agriculture (e.g., potatoes and sugarcane), forestry for timber trees, and high-value medicinal plants [<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 13" reference-link="#B13-plants-13-02544">13</button>,<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 14" reference-link="#B14-plants-13-02544">14</button>,<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 15" reference-link="#B15-plants-13-02544">15</button>]. It is able to achieve rapid clonal multiplication by utilizing either the direct or indirect organogenesis pathways [<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 16" reference-link="#B16-plants-13-02544">16</button>]. Direct organogenesis is the process where plant organs are formed directly from explanted tissues, while indirect organogenesis involves the formation of organs through an intermediate stage where de-differentiated cells (callus) are initiated and amplified before they are regenerated into full plants [<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 17" reference-link="#B17-plants-13-02544">17</button>].</div><div class="html-p">The process of micropropagation is divided into four main stages and one preparation stage.</div><div class="html-p">Pre-culture preparation—This stage involves the selection and preparation of a mother plant (source of explant collection) for TC. The health and quality of the mother plant greatly affect the success rate of culture establishment.</div><div class="html-p">Stage I—Initiation of culture: In this stage the explants (typically meristems, buds, leaves, or other tissues) are collected, sterilized and initiated on a nutrient media with Plant Growth Regulators (PGR). The main objective is to establish aseptic cultures.</div><div class="html-p">Stage II—Multiplication: The main goal in this stage is to grow the explant, into multiple shoots, commonly achieved by manipulating the balance of PGRs (between cytokinin and auxin) in the culture medium. These shoots are then separately subcultured under the same conditions. By repeating this process, exponential multiplication over multiple subcultures can be achieved.</div><div class="html-p">Stage III—Rooting: The shoots regenerated from the multiplication stage are cultured on rooting media, characterized by different auxin levels compared to previous stages, in order to stimulate root growth and obtain rooted plantlets.</div><div class="html-p">Stage IV—Acclimatization and Transfer: In the final stage of micropropagation, the rooted TC plantlets are transferred from the in vitro environment to an external environment via gradual/stepwise acclimatization. The rooted plantlets are initially acclimatized in a controlled environment like a greenhouse, then moved to the natural/production environment.</div><div class="html-p">The recent liberalization of laws and the expansion of <span class="html-italic">Cannabis</span> markets have coincided with a surge in <span class="html-italic">Cannabis</span> research, including <span class="html-italic">Cannabis</span> TC. This field has seen significant advancements in developing protocols for direct organogenesis, with a focus on different explant types and method optimizations. Key studies in this area have centered on direct organogenesis from shoot tip and nodal explants, providing valuable protocols for these specific explant types [<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 18" reference-link="#B18-plants-13-02544">18</button>,<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 19" reference-link="#B19-plants-13-02544">19</button>]. A study by Hesami and Adamek [<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 20" reference-link="#B20-plants-13-02544">20</button>] found that explants from the basal and near-basal parts of high-cannabinoid <span class="html-italic">Cannabis</span> cultivars exhibited superior multiplication and rejuvenation properties in tissue culture compared to the explants collected from the apical and middle parts of the source plant, highlighting the importance of explant selection. Kodym and Leeb [<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 21" reference-link="#B21-plants-13-02544">21</button>] developed an alternative in vitro system mimicking nursery beds using photoautotrophic micropropagation, which contributed significantly to the understanding of direct rhizogenesis essential for plantlet development. Conservation of <span class="html-italic">Cannabis</span> germplasm was also carried out using cryopreservation and synthetic seed production using in vitro explants (nodal and shoot tip) [<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 22" reference-link="#B22-plants-13-02544">22</button>,<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 23" reference-link="#B23-plants-13-02544">23</button>,<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 24" reference-link="#B24-plants-13-02544">24</button>]. Adaptations such as the reduction in the subculture period and increasing the size of the explant for greater efficiency further highlight the progress made in this field [<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 25" reference-link="#B25-plants-13-02544">25</button>].</div><div class="html-p">Despite this progress, <span class="html-italic">Cannabis</span> TC continues to face several challenges that hinder its commercial implementation. First, it is recognized that long-term culture of <span class="html-italic">Cannabis</span> in the multiplication stage can lead to vitrification (due to the gelling agent, PGR, and/or media composition [<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 26" reference-link="#B26-plants-13-02544">26</button>], and drying or browning of leaves, collectively complicating sustained multiplication efforts [<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 10" reference-link="#B10-plants-13-02544">10</button>,<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 27" reference-link="#B27-plants-13-02544">27</button>,<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 28" reference-link="#B28-plants-13-02544">28</button>]. Notably, previous reports frequently neglect long-term explant health and maintenance in TC, a gap highlighted by the lack of data on Stage II outcomes. Moreover, there is a discrepancy in terms of multiplication rate (average number of explants suitable for the next subculture produced per explant) reported, with some reporting a high number of multiple shoots in freshly initiated Stage I explants, [<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 11" reference-link="#B11-plants-13-02544">11</button>,<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 21" reference-link="#B21-plants-13-02544">21</button>,<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 28" reference-link="#B28-plants-13-02544">28</button>,<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 29" reference-link="#B29-plants-13-02544">29</button>], while others observed a low multiplication rate even when using the same protocol [<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 25" reference-link="#B25-plants-13-02544">25</button>,<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 30" reference-link="#B30-plants-13-02544">30</button>]. Finally, <span class="html-italic">Cannabis</span> is highly diverse both genetically and phenotypically; however, TC protocols have typically only been applied to a single or limited number of varieties, making it challenging to assess the broader-scale applicability of the current TC protocols across genetically distinct drug-type and hemp-type varieties [<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 31" reference-link="#B31-plants-13-02544">31</button>]. Collectively, these concerns highlight the necessity for additional research in <span class="html-italic">Cannabis</span> TC, particularly for the multiplication stage (stage II), before commercial implementation can be considered.</div><div class="html-p">This study aimed to develop an efficient and optimized <span class="html-italic">Cannabis</span> micropropagation protocol, with a specific focus on producing consistent and sufficiently high multiplication rates required for commercial adoption while maintaining vigor and health over extended periods. Additionally, the applicability of the optimized protocol was tested across a range of genetically diverse varieties. To achieve this, four type I explants (collected from the mother plant ex vitro) of a high-CBD drug-type variety were tested in four different initiation media. Successful cultures provided the five different types of stage II explants (collected from TC plantlets in vitro), which were tested across a wide range of (modified) multiplication media. The final step involved testing of the applicability of an optimized protocol on eight distinct varieties, including both drug-type and industrial hemp, including several rounds of stage II multiplication.</div></section></div><div id="Results" data-heading-anchor="1"><section id="sec2-plants-13-02544" type="results"><h2 data-nested="1">  2. Results</h2><section id="sec2dot1-plants-13-02544" type=""><h3 class="html-italic" data-nested="2">  2.1. Initiation Trials</h3><section id="sec2dot1dot1-plants-13-02544" type=""><h4 class="" data-nested="3">  2.1.1. Surface-Sterilization</h4><div class="html-p">Fungal contamination (<a href="#plants-13-02544-f0A2" class="html-fig">Figure A2</a>a,b) on stage I explants was observed to manifest between the fourth- and fourteenth-day post-inoculation, and bacterial contamination (<a href="#plants-13-02544-f0A2" class="html-fig">Figure A2</a>c,d) appeared after 2–3 weeks in culture, resulting in substantial attrition rates. Without carbendazim, the shortest exposure time (10 min) to 1% sodium hypochlorite (NaOCl) treatment alone resulted in 100% contamination, whereas prolonged exposures (45 min or more) caused 75% or more tissue death (<a href="#plants-13-02544-t001" class="html-table">Table 1</a>). Carbendazim treatment at 0.5 or 1 gm/L significantly reduced fungal contamination; however, it also significantly increased tissue death and reduced the viability of the explants, with the number of suitable explants for subculture dropping at 30 min 15 NaOCl treatment from 55% with no carbendazim to 10% in 1 gm/L carbendazim. Thus, due to these detrimental effects on tissue health, carbendazim treatment was not included in the selected sterilization procedure. The 1% NaOCl treatment for 30 min without carbendazim significantly reduced fungal contamination compared to shorter exposure times, while showing significantly less tissue death than longer exposures or exposures including carbendazim, leading to 55% of the explants being suitable for further TC (<a href="#plants-13-02544-t001" class="html-table">Table 1</a>). Thus, the 30 min without carbendazim treatment was used as a sterilization treatment in an active subsequent experiment.</div><div class="html-table-wrap" id="plants-13-02544-t001" data-position="float">
  <div class="html-table_wrap_td">
    <div class="html-tablepopup html-tablepopup-link" href="#table_body_display_plants-13-02544-t001">
        <img src="https://pub.mdpi-res.com/img/table.png" alt="" role="presentation" width="138" height="79" loading="lazy">
       
    </div>

  </div>
  <div class="html-table_wrap_discription">
    <b>Table 1.</b>
    (<b>a</b>) Effectiveness of different surface sterilization treatments using different 1% NaOCl exposure times and carbendazim concentrations (n = 20). (<b>b</b>) Main effects of the surface sterilization treatments experiment and statistical significance of the observed differences in contamination, tissue death, and number of explants suitable for subculture.
  </div>
</div></section><section id="sec2dot1dot2-plants-13-02544" type=""><h4 class="" data-nested="3">  2.1.2. Stage I Explant Type</h4><div class="html-p">Fungal contamination was observed to be significantly less in meristems and microshoot tips compared to shoot tips and nodal explants (<a href="#plants-13-02544-t002" class="html-table">Table 2</a>). Bacterial contamination appeared to be higher for nodal explants (40% bacterial) and shoot tips (25% bacterial) compared to microshoot tips and meristems. Similarly, meristem explants appeared to have higher vitrification and stronger callus growth than microshoot tips. As the microshoot tip explant showed significantly lower contamination than the shoot tip and nodal explants, and appeared to have lower vitrification than the meristem, microshoot tip explants were selected for all subsequent culture establishments.</div><div class="html-table-wrap" id="plants-13-02544-t002" data-position="float">
  <div class="html-table_wrap_td">
    <div class="html-tablepopup html-tablepopup-link" href="#table_body_display_plants-13-02544-t002">
        <img src="https://pub.mdpi-res.com/img/table.png" alt="" role="presentation" width="138" height="79" loading="lazy">
       
    </div>

  </div>
  <div class="html-table_wrap_discription">
    <b>Table 2.</b>
    Analysis of concomitant response to different stage I explant types (n = 20).
  </div>
</div></section><section id="sec2dot1dot3-plants-13-02544" type=""><h4 class="" data-nested="3">  2.1.3. Initiation Media Comparison</h4><div class="html-p">The microshoot tip explants in Initiation Media 3 (IM3), comprising Murashige and Skoog (MS) media [<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 32" reference-link="#B32-plants-13-02544">32</button>,<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 33" reference-link="#B33-plants-13-02544">33</button>] with 0.48 mg/L meta-Topolin (mT) [<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 11" reference-link="#B11-plants-13-02544">11</button>], exhibited the best growth with rapid increases in size and length, and IM3 was selected as the initiation media for all subsequent culture establishments (<a href="#plants-13-02544-t003" class="html-table">Table 3</a>). The microshoot tip explants in IM1, MS media with 0.11 mg/L Thidiazuron (TDZ) [<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 29" reference-link="#B29-plants-13-02544">29</button>], exhibited stunted growth with dark green leaves, and the microshoot tip explants in IM2, MS media with 0.2 mg/L TDZ and 0.1 mg/L 1-naphthaleneacetic acid (NAA) [<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 34" reference-link="#B34-plants-13-02544">34</button>], appeared more prone to vitrification and callus. The same explant type in IM4, Driver and Kuniyuki Walnut (DKW) [<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 35" reference-link="#B35-plants-13-02544">35</button>] media with 0.11 mg/L TDZ [<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 31" reference-link="#B31-plants-13-02544">31</button>], showed light green leaves and media browning near the base of the explant, indicating unhealthy explants.</div><div class="html-table-wrap" id="plants-13-02544-t003" data-position="float">
  <div class="html-table_wrap_td">
    <div class="html-tablepopup html-tablepopup-link" href="#table_body_display_plants-13-02544-t003">
        <img src="https://pub.mdpi-res.com/img/table.png" alt="" role="presentation" width="138" height="79" loading="lazy">
       
    </div>

  </div>
  <div class="html-table_wrap_discription">
    <b>Table 3.</b>
    Analysis of optimal response to different initiation media (IM) (n = 20).
  </div>
</div></section></section><section id="sec2dot2-plants-13-02544" type=""><h3 class="html-italic" data-nested="2">  2.2. Multiplication Trials</h3><section id="sec2dot2dot1-plants-13-02544" type=""><h4 class="" data-nested="3">  2.2.1. Media Trial 0 (MT0)</h4><div class="html-p">The effect of PGRs constitutes a key aspect of the multiplication (Stage II), as it is added to encourage the growth of multiple shoots from a single explant. In this experiment, the explants from IM3 were subcultured in six different media to assess the effect of the presence and absence of meta-topolin (mT) based on the vitrification level, a potential negative consequence (<a href="#plants-13-02544-f001" class="html-fig">Figure 1</a>). It was observed that the media with 0.5 mg/L meta-topolin (MM2 + H, MM8 + H and MM9 + H) showed significantly higher rates of vitrification compared to their PGR-free counterparts MM2, MM8, and MM9, respectively. The control group (MM2), despite having no PGRs, displayed a higher level of vitrification compared to MM8 and MM9.</div><div class="html-fig-wrap" id="plants-13-02544-f001" data-position="float">
  <div class="html-fig_img" style="max-width:400px;">
    <div class="html-figpopup html-figpopup-link" href="#fig_body_display_plants-13-02544-f001">
      <img data-large="https://mdpi-res.com/plants/plants-13-02544/article_deploy/html/images/plants-13-02544-g001.png" data-original="https://mdpi-res.com/plants/plants-13-02544/article_deploy/html/images/plants-13-02544-g001.png" alt="" src="https://mdpi-res.com/plants/plants-13-02544/article_deploy/html/images/plants-13-02544-g001-550.jpg" title="" role="presentation" style="max-height:400px;min-height:150px;object-fit:contain;" width="550" height="268" loading="lazy">
      
    </div>

  </div>
  <div class="html-fig_description">

      Click here to enlarge figure
    </a></p> -->

  </div>
</div></section><section id="sec2dot2dot2-plants-13-02544" type=""><h4 class="" data-nested="3">  2.2.2. Media Trial 1 (MT1)</h4><div class="html-p">To analyze the effect of media composition and phase 2 explant type in the multiplication stage, the performance of five different phase 2 explant types was assessed on 11 different media modifications. It was observed that the explants in treatments with DKW (MM10) [<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 35" reference-link="#B35-plants-13-02544">35</button>] and a combination of ½ DKW and ½ MS (MM11) [<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 32" reference-link="#B32-plants-13-02544">32</button>] exhibited signs of severe distress 2–3 weeks after inoculation, including the presence of light green or bleached leaves, stunted growth or no growth, and a notable browning of the media near the bases of the explants. As they ultimately led to tissue death, MM10 and MM11 were discontinued shortly after the preliminary testing stage. The log-transformed data of height showed near-normal distribution (even though it failed the Shapiro–Wilk test). The predicted treatment height means (log-transformed) showed that the explants in MM8 and MM9 were statistically the same and superior to all other media composition except MM5, which showed comparable results to MM1, MM2, and MM6 (<a href="#plants-13-02544-f002" class="html-fig">Figure 2</a>a). Thus, data for MM8 and MM9 were pooled to assess the effect of phase 2 explant types (<a href="#plants-13-02544-f003" class="html-fig">Figure 3</a>). Height data exhibited a decrease from E1 to E5 (<a href="#plants-13-02544-f002" class="html-fig">Figure 2</a>b), with E1 and E2 showing significant differences from E4 and E5, whereas E3 did not exhibit significant differences from the E1, E2, and E4 explant types.</div><div class="html-fig-wrap" id="plants-13-02544-f002" data-position="float">
  <div class="html-fig_img" style="max-width:400px;">
    <div class="html-figpopup html-figpopup-link" href="#fig_body_display_plants-13-02544-f002">
      <img data-large="https://mdpi-res.com/plants/plants-13-02544/article_deploy/html/images/plants-13-02544-g002.png" data-original="https://mdpi-res.com/plants/plants-13-02544/article_deploy/html/images/plants-13-02544-g002.png" alt="" src="https://mdpi-res.com/plants/plants-13-02544/article_deploy/html/images/plants-13-02544-g002-550.jpg" title="" role="presentation" style="max-height:400px;min-height:150px;object-fit:contain;" width="550" height="220" loading="lazy">

</div></section><section id="sec2dot2dot3-plants-13-02544" type=""><h4 class="" data-nested="3">  2.2.3. Media Trial 2 (MT2)</h4><div class="html-p">In an attempt to further optimize the media, the two best-performing phase 2 explant types (E1 and E2—pooled for analysis) were tested against additional media formulations based on the two best performing media in MT1 (MM8 and MM9) (<a href="#plants-13-02544-f004" class="html-fig">Figure 4</a>). MM8 and MM12 showed the healthiest explants, followed by MM9, MM13, and MM14, and the rest showed reasonably healthy explants. For height and multiplication rate, MM12 outperformed the rest of the media compositions. MM8 and MM12 showed better results for rooting. In the indexing of the media, it was observed that MM12 outperformed the other treatments, with M8 ranked second (<a href="#plants-13-02544-t004" class="html-table">Table 4</a>).</div><div class="html-fig-wrap" id="plants-13-02544-f004" data-position="float">
  <div class="html-fig_img" style="max-width:400px;">
    <div class="html-figpopup html-figpopup-link" href="#fig_body_display_plants-13-02544-f004">

  </div>
</div><div class="html-p">A correlation dot-plot was used to visualize the correlations among the traits measured for MT2 (<a href="#plants-13-02544-f0A3" class="html-fig">Figure A3</a>). Vitrification was negatively correlated with height, health, rooting, and multiplication rate, indicating a tendency for these parameters to decrease as vitrification increased. In contrast, positive correlations were observed between health, height, multiplication rate, and rooting, with height and multiplication rate being very highly correlated.</div></section><section id="sec2dot2dot4-plants-13-02544" type=""><h4 class="" data-nested="3">  2.2.4. Losses Due to Contamination in Multiplication</h4><div class="html-p">Endophytic contamination frequently appeared in stage II explants during the multiplication stage (stage II—3 to 4 weeks), causing loss of culture (<a href="#plants-13-02544-f0A2" class="html-fig">Figure A2</a>e,f), and also during root development (<a href="#plants-13-02544-f0A2" class="html-fig">Figure A2</a>g,h). This led to the reduction in the multiplication rate during the multiplication stage.</div></section></section><section id="sec2dot3-plants-13-02544" type=""><h3 class="html-italic" data-nested="2">  2.3. Acclimatization</h3><div class="html-p">Most of the explants rooted in the multiplication media (<a href="#plants-13-02544-f0A4" class="html-fig">Figure A4</a>) and showed a 90% survival rate (16 plants out of 18 transferred) when transferred to coco peat plugs. The plants in coco peat showed normal morphological growth. After successful acclimatization, they were successfully transferred to a greenhouse.</div></section><section id="sec2dot4-plants-13-02544" type=""><h3 class="html-italic" data-nested="2">  2.4. Applicability of the Optimized Protocol on Eight Diverse Varieties</h3><div class="html-p">Loss during the initiation stage was high at 90–95% for all varieties, except for IPK-CAN-44 (~75%) (<a href="#plants-13-02544-t005" class="html-table">Table 5</a>). The multiplication rate for each subculture varied across the different subcultures and varieties, with the rate remaining within a range of 1 to 4. In the next subculture, the multiplication rate dropped to 1 to 3. The rooted explants after the three-multiplication round were successfully acclimatized and transferred to a greenhouse.</div><div class="html-table-wrap" id="plants-13-02544-t005" data-position="float">
  <div class="html-table_wrap_td">
    <div class="html-tablepopup html-tablepopup-link" href="#table_body_display_plants-13-02544-t005">
        <img src="https://pub.mdpi-res.com/img/table.png" alt="" role="presentation" width="138" height="79" loading="lazy">
       
    </div>

  </div>
  <div class="html-table_wrap_discription">
    <b>Table 5.</b>
    Different varieties tried for the optimized media from initiation and multiplication trials. Initiation number 20 repeated 3 times (n = 20 × 3). The 1st subculture in 3 weeks, the second and third subcultures in 4 week intervals.
  </div>
</div></section></section></div><div id="Discussion" data-heading-anchor="1"><section id="sec3-plants-13-02544" type="discussion"><h2 data-nested="1">  3. Discussion</h2><section id="sec3dot1-plants-13-02544" type=""><h3 class="html-italic" data-nested="2">  3.1. Surface Sterilization</h3><div class="html-p">The results of this study highlighted the fine balance between effectiveness of sterilization conditions and preserving tissue health when performing surface sterilizations. When using bleach alone, a 30 min 1% NaOCl treatment was identified as optimal, with longer treatments causing significantly higher mortality and shorter treatments resulting in too high contamination (<a href="#plants-13-02544-t001" class="html-table">Table 1</a>). A 10 min treatment at 1.5, 3.0, or 4.5% NaOCl concentrations was reported to be effective for surface disinfecting explants collected from clean and pest-free growth chambers of <span class="html-italic">Cannabis</span> plants with low endophytic microbes; however, all failed to remove contamination in explant-collected <span class="html-italic">Cannabis</span> plants grown in a greenhouse [<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 36" reference-link="#B36-plants-13-02544">36</button>]. While for species such as <span class="html-italic">Sideritis Raeseri Boiss</span>, <span class="html-italic">Heldr. Subsp. Raeseri,</span> and <span class="html-italic">Peperomia pellucida</span>, carbendazim was found to be suitable for surface sterilization and media incorporation [<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 37" reference-link="#B37-plants-13-02544">37</button>,<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 38" reference-link="#B38-plants-13-02544">38</button>], in <span class="html-italic">Cannabis</span>, carbendazim was found to markedly increase explant mortality already at a concentration of 0.5 gm/L, with higher mortality rates at higher concentrations (<a href="#plants-13-02544-t001" class="html-table">Table 1</a>). This might be due to its inhibitory effects on metabolic processes, as treatments with carbendazim had considerably higher tissue damage than treatments without it. Similar antagonistic effects were observed in tobacco plants when carbendazim was used in excess, which inhibited phenolic metabolism [<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 39" reference-link="#B39-plants-13-02544">39</button>].</div><div class="html-p">Although surface sterilization was able to markedly reduce the contamination, it failed to eliminate the <span class="html-italic">Cannabis</span> endophytes. <span class="html-italic">Cannabis</span> endophytes are believed to have the potential to enhance <span class="html-italic">Cannabis</span> growth and cannabinoid yields, though the details of their interaction are not fully understood [<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 40" reference-link="#B40-plants-13-02544">40</button>]. However, endophytes also have been identified as likely attributing to a resurgence of contamination observed several weeks after an aseptic culture appears to have been established [<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 41" reference-link="#B41-plants-13-02544">41</button>]. As it was found that endophytes could not be removed via surface sterilization, they likely need to be managed via explant type selection and/or subculturing approaches.</div></section><section id="sec3dot2-plants-13-02544" type=""><h3 class="html-italic" data-nested="2">  3.2. Initiation</h3><div class="html-p">Among the four initiation media tested, IM3 was selected as it appeared to produce explants with rapid and sustained growth. Microshoot tip explants (less than 0.5 cm in size and near meristematic tissue) exhibited the best overall results, characterized by very low levels of contamination, vitrification, and callus formation (<a href="#plants-13-02544-t002" class="html-table">Table 2</a>). The low levels of contamination might be due to a low level of endophytes in actively growing shoot tips, as previously reported in <span class="html-italic">Oryza sativa</span> [<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 42" reference-link="#B42-plants-13-02544">42</button>]. Shoot tip and nodal explants were clearly more affected by fungal and bacterial contamination (<a href="#plants-13-02544-t002" class="html-table">Table 2</a>), possibly as a result of a heavier endophytic load as observed previously [<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 25" reference-link="#B25-plants-13-02544">25</button>,<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 28" reference-link="#B28-plants-13-02544">28</button>,<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 43" reference-link="#B43-plants-13-02544">43</button>]. Meristematic explants, while free from contamination, showed increased vitrification and callus formation, potentially causing unwanted soma-clonal variation, likely due to their high metabolic activity and mostly undifferentiated state [<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 44" reference-link="#B44-plants-13-02544">44</button>,<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 45" reference-link="#B45-plants-13-02544">45</button>,<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 46" reference-link="#B46-plants-13-02544">46</button>,<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 47" reference-link="#B47-plants-13-02544">47</button>]. Thus, it is advised to use microshoot tip explants for initiation stage 1 TC. However, a decline in health was observed after 2–3 subculture rounds in this medium, characterized by decreased growth and increased vitrification, as evidenced by light green, translucent, and brittle leaves. Similar observations were reported by Lubell-Brand and Kurtz [<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 8" reference-link="#B8-plants-13-02544">8</button>]. This decline in health over time likely indicated excessive humidity and an imbalance between the supplied and endogenous PGRs that exacerbated over time, suggesting that a different media composition and PGR formulation for the multiplication stage (Stage II) would be of benefit.</div></section><section id="sec3dot3-plants-13-02544" type=""><h3 class="html-italic" data-nested="2">  3.3. PGRs and Vitrification</h3><div class="html-p">The use of the PGR meta-topolin in stage II multiplication media was found to negatively affect <span class="html-italic">Cannabis</span> TC by significantly increasing vitrification (<a href="#plants-13-02544-f001" class="html-fig">Figure 1</a>), which is in contrast to other crops like bananas, ornamental trees, and coconuts where they enhanced growth and multiplication [<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 12" reference-link="#B12-plants-13-02544">12</button>,<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 48" reference-link="#B48-plants-13-02544">48</button>,<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 49" reference-link="#B49-plants-13-02544">49</button>,<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 50" reference-link="#B50-plants-13-02544">50</button>]. This vitrification was likely the result of an excessive presence of cytokinins in the media formulated with meta-topolin, which, especially when combined with ammonium, has been found to contribute to vitrification in <span class="html-italic">Cannabis</span>, causing leaf narrowing, leaf curling, and stunted growth [<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 25" reference-link="#B25-plants-13-02544">25</button>,<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 43" reference-link="#B43-plants-13-02544">43</button>]. Previous studies mostly relied on the addition of PGRs to induce multiple shoot cultures, where each shoot could be subcultured to achieve a high multiplication rate in an effort to replicate the success seen in other crops [<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 36" reference-link="#B36-plants-13-02544">36</button>,<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 51" reference-link="#B51-plants-13-02544">51</button>,<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 52" reference-link="#B52-plants-13-02544">52</button>]. Similarly, the PGR Thidiazuron (TDZ) increased fresh weights, shoot numbers, and lateral nodes, but it also increased vitrification and callus formation, leading to physiological abnormalities and multiplication losses of up to 90% [<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 36" reference-link="#B36-plants-13-02544">36</button>,<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 53" reference-link="#B53-plants-13-02544">53</button>]. Notably, the control group (MM2), despite having no meta-topolin, displayed a higher level of vitrification compared to MM8 and MM9, indicating that while PGRs contribute to vitrification, they are not the sole influencing factor.</div></section><section id="sec3dot4-plants-13-02544" type=""><h3 class="html-italic" data-nested="2">  3.4. Multiplication Via Single Main Shoot Cultures</h3><div class="html-p">For multiplication, the exclusion of PGRs (cytokinins) in the media to reduce/avoid vitrification meant that, instead of a multiple shoot culture, the aim was to grow a single, high-quality main shoot. Consequently, sections of the main shoot would need to serve as explants for subsequent subcultures to achieve a multiplication rate greater than 1. To manage growth constraints imposed by the container’s height, the subculture period was consistently set at four weeks.</div><div class="html-p">In the multiplication stage, primary and secondary shoot tips (E1 and E2) performed significantly better than other types (E4 and E5), with no differences between E1 and E2. (<a href="#plants-13-02544-f002" class="html-fig">Figure 2</a>). E3 was not significantly different from either group. This enhanced performance of shoot tips close to the top might be attributed to the high levels of endogenous PGRs present in the actively growing parts of these explants. Additionally, the improved outcomes could also be linked to apical dominance exhibited by in vitro <span class="html-italic">Cannabis</span> explants [<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 25" reference-link="#B25-plants-13-02544">25</button>,<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 30" reference-link="#B30-plants-13-02544">30</button>,<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 43" reference-link="#B43-plants-13-02544">43</button>].</div></section><section id="sec3dot5-plants-13-02544" type=""><h3 class="html-italic" data-nested="2">  3.5. Multiplication Media</h3><div class="html-p">MS media was selected as the base for the multiplication media as it has been widely used for <span class="html-italic">Cannabis</span> micropropagation [<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 11" reference-link="#B11-plants-13-02544">11</button>,<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 25" reference-link="#B25-plants-13-02544">25</button>,<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 54" reference-link="#B54-plants-13-02544">54</button>]. However, unmodified MS media (MM2) in absence of PGRs caused increased vitrification and reduced performance (<a href="#plants-13-02544-f002" class="html-fig">Figure 2</a>). Inadequate supply of calcium in media has been reported to lead to accumulation of phenolic compounds and cell death, while the level of calcium in the media also directly affects auxin transport and ethylene production and could encourage the shoot tip necrosis and vitrification [<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 55" reference-link="#B55-plants-13-02544">55</button>]. It is thus likely that MS media alone is not able to fulfill the nutritional requirements of a vigorous feeder like <span class="html-italic">Cannabis</span>. Lubell-Brand, Kurtz [<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 8" reference-link="#B8-plants-13-02544">8</button>] reported that the addition of 500 mg/L ammonium nitrate in modified shoot multiplication media with vitamins and a mixture containing calcium chloride (anhydrous), magnesium sulfate (anhydrous), and monobasic potassium phosphate (also called mesos), effectively doubled the multiplication rate by invigorating the <span class="html-italic">Cannabis</span> explants for at least 12 weeks following the initiation step. The addition of 1 gm/L calcium gluconate to the TC media was also reported to efficiently control shoot necrosis in primocane-fruiting raspberry micropropagation, where in vitro shoot necrosis was a major problem [<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 56" reference-link="#B56-plants-13-02544">56</button>]. In this study, the addition of calcium nitrate alone enhanced growth and height, while calcium gluconate alone improved explant quality by decreasing vitrification; however, their combined use yielded the optimal results (<a href="#plants-13-02544-f004" class="html-fig">Figure 4</a>).</div><div class="html-p">In this study, ferric-sodium EDDHA (Fe-EDDHA) was found ineffective in increasing shoot induction and multiplication or improving the quality of the <span class="html-italic">Cannabis</span> explants. In contrast, the explants on media containing Fe-EDDHA were observed to perform poorly compared to those without it. Although the use of an Fe-EDDHA with BAP was reported to increase shoot induction and multiplication in species such as <span class="html-italic">Baptisia australis</span>, <span class="html-italic">Rubus fruticosus,</span> and <span class="html-italic">Hibiscus rosa-sinensis</span> by either neutrally or negatively influencing the strong stimulus of BAP on the induction of apical buds and nodal sections [<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 57" reference-link="#B57-plants-13-02544">57</button>,<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 58" reference-link="#B58-plants-13-02544">58</button>,<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 59" reference-link="#B59-plants-13-02544">59</button>], this was not observed in our study.</div><div class="html-p">The agar concentration from 7 gm/L in MT1 was increased to 9.5 gm/L in MT2 to address the issue of high vitrification through the reduction in humidity inside the container. A similar addition was reported to improve the health of <span class="html-italic">Cannabis</span> TC and reduce vitrification [<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 60" reference-link="#B60-plants-13-02544">60</button>]. Although not tested separately, the best performing media had the increased agar concentration suggesting that the higher agar concentration, may be of benefit.</div></section><section id="sec3dot6-plants-13-02544" type=""><h3 class="html-italic" data-nested="2">  3.6. Traits to Measure Explant Quality</h3><div class="html-p">The correlation among observed parameters in MT2 showed that height, rooting, health, and multiplication rate were positively correlated with each other and negatively correlated with vitrification (<a href="#plants-13-02544-f0A3" class="html-fig">Figure A3</a>). Height showed positive correlation with health (0.69), rooting (0.82), multiplication rate (0.87), and negative correlation with vitrification (−0.51). Therefore, it proved to be a reliable indicator and could be used as the primary selection trait, as was performed in MT1. Vitrification showed negative co-relation with health (−0.77), rooting (−0.61), and multiplication rate (−0.47).</div></section><section id="sec3dot7-plants-13-02544" type=""><h3 class="html-italic" data-nested="2">  3.7. Applicability</h3><div class="html-p">Eight diverse varieties were tested with the best-performing initiation method (<a href="#sec4dot3dot1-plants-13-02544" class="html-sec">Section 4.3.1</a>) and multiplication method (<a href="#sec4dot3dot2-plants-13-02544" class="html-sec">Section 4.3.2</a>). Losses during initiation were higher than expected at 45% (<a href="#plants-13-02544-t005" class="html-table">Table 5</a>). The low survival rates during initiation could be attributed to high initial contamination from the presence of numerous endophytes typical of unimproved germplasm. The contamination-free explants were successfully maintained through three subcultures in MM12 media. The different varieties showed different multiplication rates in the same multiplication medium ranging from 1 to 4 in the second subculture to 1 to 3 in the third subculture. As a comparison, the multiplication rate of the commercial CBD line used for the method development, which went through at least four subculture rounds before being used in the multiplication trials, showed a multiplication rate ranging between 4 and 6 per explant in MM12 media (<a href="#plants-13-02544-f004" class="html-fig">Figure 4</a>). This variation in multiplication rate in different varieties illustrates the genotype-dependent nature of <span class="html-italic">Cannabis</span> tissue culture, which has also been reported by others [<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 30" reference-link="#B30-plants-13-02544">30</button>]. The optimized protocol’s success in integrating all eight lines into tissue culture and being able to maintain them in the multiplication stage was considered a significant step forward. However, depending on the genotype, further increases in the multiplication rate may be crucial for successful commercial production. This study was primarily focused on the multiplication phase of micropropagation, and as such, it is limited in addressing the broader issues of vitrification and contamination. Further research is necessary to better understand these effects and their potential correlations with other factors.</div></section><section id="sec3dot8-plants-13-02544" type=""><h3 class="html-italic" data-nested="2">  3.8. Conclusions</h3><div class="html-p">The detailed investigation of the initiation and multiplication stages of <span class="html-italic">Cannabis</span> micropropagation yielded several significant findings. Bleach-based surface sterilization proved adequate for surface decontamination but was ineffective against endophytes. The selection of the microshoot tip as the phase 1 explant type significantly enhanced growth and helped reduce contamination and vitrification compared to most other explant types. The use of the PGR meta-topolin was found to be detrimental in multiplication (stage II) due to the increase in vitrification, necessitating a shift in focus from multiple shoot cultures to growing single main shoot cultures in this stage. The stage II explant types with shoot tips (E1 and E2) produced optimal results across multiple subcultures. The inclusion of calcium nitrate and calcium gluconate in the multiplication media was determined to enhance the overall <span class="html-italic">Cannabis</span> TC outcome. This optimized protocol was successfully applied to eight different varieties, which demonstrated its effectiveness and adaptability across a diverse range of <span class="html-italic">Cannabis</span> genetics for conservation purposes, although genotype-dependency meant that further increases in the multiplication rate may be crucial for successful commercial production.</div></section></section></div><div id="Materials_and_Methods" data-heading-anchor="1"><section id="sec4-plants-13-02544" type=""><h2 data-nested="1">  4. Materials and Methods</h2><section id="sec4dot1-plants-13-02544" type=""><h3 class="html-italic" data-nested="2">  4.1. Genetic Resources and Mother Plant Cultivation</h3><div class="html-p">The preliminary initiation and multiplication trials were conducted with a high-CBD cultivar cultivated at the Cymra Life Science Northern Rivers campus (Northern Rivers, NSW, Australia), who were appropriately licensed to hold the material. Mature <span class="html-italic">Cannabis</span> mother plants growing in a greenhouse served as the explant source. The TCs at Cymra were maintained in a rack fitted with slimline seamless TC linkable SL9706-4/22 LED tube lights from SAL, Australia with ~3000 lux light intensity, growth room at temperature 25 ± 2 °C and 60% relative humidity.</div><div class="html-p">The genotypes selected for variety trialing were mainly from Eurasian origins obtained from the “IPK genebank collection” of the Leibniz Institute of Plant Genetics and Crop Plant Research (Leibniz-Institut für Pflanzengenetik und Kulturpflanzenforschung, IPK—Germany) and were grown at the Southern Cross University Lismore campus (Lismore, NSW, Australia). The experimental work on the high THC <span class="html-italic">Cannabis</span> varieties at Southern Cross University (Lismore, Australia) was conducted under an authority granted to Prof. Bronwyn Barkla of Southern Cross University (SCU), issued by the New South Wales Ministry of Health (Australia). The experimental work on the low-THC industrial hemp varieties at Southern Cross University (Lismore, Australia) was conducted under hemp license number 52204 issued by the NSW Department of Primary Industries (Orange, Australia).</div><div class="html-p">Fifty seeds from the varieties were germinated, and 20 seedlings were selected and grown in seed trays that served as the explant source. The plants were grown on premix potting soil mix with 70% coco, 30% perlite, and 3.4 g/L osmocoat exact 3–4 in a grow room. The TCs were maintained in a Versatile Environmental Test Chamber (Sanyo model MLR-350T) at temperature 25 + 2 °C and ~3000 lux.</div></section><section id="sec4dot2-plants-13-02544" type=""><h3 class="html-italic" data-nested="2">  4.2. Initiation</h3><section id="sec4dot2dot1-plants-13-02544" type=""><h4 class="" data-nested="3">  4.2.1. Surface-Sterilization Trial</h4><div class="html-p">For establishing the TC, phase 1 explants were taken from the mother plants (ex vitro) and surface-sterilized (<a href="#plants-13-02544-f0A1" class="html-fig">Figure A1</a>a–c) before being inoculated in initiation media. An initial surface-sterilization trial assessed the effectiveness of 1% NaOCl (premium chlorine bleach 4%) sterilization treatment, with and without carbendazim (a broad-spectrum fungicide), on reducing contamination and minimizing tissue death after four weeks post-initiation. Excised stage I explants (shoot tip and nodes) were washed in autoclave distilled water with a few drops of undiluted Tween-20 to remove the adhering dust particles. The washed explants were immersed in 1% NaOCl in a 1 L Schott bottle and rotated in a rotary shaker at 150 rpm for 10 to 60 min. Next, treated explants were washed 5 times with autoclaved distilled water. Selected explants were treated with 0.5 gm/L or 1 gm/L concentration of carbendazim for 15 min and washed as above before being trimmed and inoculated onto MS media with 0.5 mg/L meta-topolin. For each treatment group, twenty explants were used. Explants with contamination, tissue death, and suitable for subculture (stage II) were measured as binary traits (yes/no).</div></section><section id="sec4dot2dot2-plants-13-02544" type=""><h4 class="" data-nested="3">  4.2.2. Stage I Explant Type Used in Initiation Trial</h4><div class="html-p">Four different types of Stage I explant types, meristem, microshoot tip, shoot tip, and nodal segments (<a href="#plants-13-02544-f0A1" class="html-fig">Figure A1</a>d–g), were surface-sterilized with a 30 min 1% NaOCl treatment and initiated on media as described under 4.2.1. Each Stage I explant type treatment consisted of 20 units (4 explant/700 mL container). Vitrification was recorded as presence (<a href="#plants-13-02544-f005" class="html-fig">Figure 5</a>e score 5 to 2) or absent (<a href="#plants-13-02544-f005" class="html-fig">Figure 5</a>e score 1). Contamination and vitrification of the explants were recorded 4 weeks after initiation as binary traits (yes/no).</div><div class="html-fig-wrap" id="plants-13-02544-f005" data-position="float">
  <div class="html-fig_img" style="max-width:400px;">
    <div class="html-figpopup html-figpopup-link" href="#fig_body_display_plants-13-02544-f005">
      <img data-large="https://mdpi-res.com/plants/plants-13-02544/article_deploy/html/images/plants-13-02544-g005.png" data-original="https://mdpi-res.com/plants/plants-13-02544/article_deploy/html/images/plants-13-02544-g005.png" alt="" src="https://mdpi-res.com/plants/plants-13-02544/article_deploy/html/images/plants-13-02544-g005-550.jpg" title="" role="presentation" style="max-height:400px;min-height:150px;object-fit:contain;" width="550" height="680" loading="lazy">
      
    </div>

  </div>
  <div class="html-fig_description">
    <b>Figure 5.</b>
      The traits measured in the multiplication trials (MT0-2). (<b>a</b>) plant height (cm), (<b>b</b>) multiplication rate (count), (<b>c</b>) plant rooting (score 5–1 based on the density of the roots from most densely rooted to no rooting), (<b>d</b>) plant health (score from 5 to 1, ranging from best to worst), and (<b>e</b>) vitrification (scored from 5 to 1, ranging from highly vitrified to no vitrification).
<!--     <p><a class="html-figpopup" href="#fig_body_display_plants-13-02544-f005">
      Click here to enlarge figure
    </a></p> -->

  </div>
</div></section><section id="sec4dot2dot3-plants-13-02544" type=""><h4 class="" data-nested="3">  4.2.3. Stage II Explant Types Used in Multiplication Trials</h4><div class="html-p">After 4 weeks, the stage I explants from initiation media that had grown without showing any sign of contamination during initiation were subcultured in MS basal media with MS vitamin mix without hormone for another 2–3 subculture rounds to generate stage II explants needed to assess the influence of explant type on multiplication (<a href="#plants-13-02544-f002" class="html-fig">Figure 2</a> and <a href="#plants-13-02544-f004" class="html-fig">Figure 4</a>). The stage II explants utilized in the multiplication trials were: E1—primary shoot tips usually characterized by multiple nodes and relatively short internodal segments; E2—secondary shoot tips usually characterized by fewer nodes and longer internodal segments; E3—secondary shoot tip with primary node; E4—small secondary shoot tip with primary node; and E5—primary nodes with or without emerging bud.</div></section></section><section id="sec4dot3-plants-13-02544" type=""><h3 class="html-italic" data-nested="2">  4.3. Media Composition</h3><section id="sec4dot3dot1-plants-13-02544" type=""><h4 class="" data-nested="3">  4.3.1. Media Compositions Used in Initiation Trial</h4><div class="html-p">Stage I explant types as described for <a href="#sec4dot2dot2-plants-13-02544" class="html-sec">Section 4.2.2</a> were surface-sterilized with a 30 min 1% bleach treatment and initiated as described under <a href="#sec4dot2dot1-plants-13-02544" class="html-sec">Section 4.2.1</a>. on four different media as listed in <a href="#plants-13-02544-t006" class="html-table">Table 6</a>. Three initiation media were based on MS, while one was based on DKW. The media were prepared by making stock solutions. Stock solution A (all macronutrients), stock B (micronutrient), stock C (for iron), and stock D (vitamins) were obtained from ChemSupply, Australia. Micropropagation-grade agar from the PhytoTech lab, Australia, was used as a gelling agent. A total of 30 gm/L (3%) sucrose from ChemSupply, Australia, was added. The pH of the media was adjusted to 5.7–5.8 using 1 mM of NaOH and subsequently autoclaved for 20 min at 121 °C, and 18 psi. PGRs (NAA, mT, and TDZ) sourced from Phytotech, Australia, were filter sterilized using a millex-GP filter unit (sterile) from Sigma-Aldrich, St. Louis, MO, USA, and added to media after autoclaving. Media were prepared in 1 L Schott bottles and poured in 30 mL sterile initiation tubes in a laminar hood. Each media composition consisted of 20 replicates (1 explant/30 mL tubes, n = 20). Contamination and vitrification were recorded 4 weeks after initiation, similar to <a href="#sec4dot2dot2-plants-13-02544" class="html-sec">Section 4.2.2</a>.</div><div class="html-table-wrap" id="plants-13-02544-t006" data-position="float">
  <div class="html-table_wrap_td">
    <div class="html-tablepopup html-tablepopup-link" href="#table_body_display_plants-13-02544-t006">
        <img src="https://pub.mdpi-res.com/img/table.png" alt="" role="presentation" width="138" height="79" loading="lazy">
       
    </div>

  </div>
  <div class="html-table_wrap_discription">
    <b>Table 6.</b>
    The 4 types of initiation media (IM) used for culture establishment.
  </div>
</div></section><section id="sec4dot3dot2-plants-13-02544" type=""><h4 class="" data-nested="3">  4.3.2. Media Compositions Used in Multiplication Trials</h4><div class="html-p">The media compositions tested in multiplication trials included half-strength MS medium, standard MS medium, modified MS medium enhanced with the (0.1 mM = 45.32 mg/L) Fe-EDDHA, CAS No. 16455-61-1, instead of (0.1 mM = 37.26 mg/L) ferric-sodium EDTA, DKW medium, and a combination of ½ DKW and ½ MS media (<a href="#plants-13-02544-t007" class="html-table">Table 7</a>). Modifications to the basal media involved calcium gluconate (CAS No. 66905-23-5), calcium nitrate (CAS No. 13477-34-4), and agar (CAS No. 9002-18-0) sourced from Phytotech Australia. The media preparation followed the same procedure as the initiation media and was poured into a 700 mL sterilized round plastic container in a laminar hood.</div><div class="html-table-wrap" id="plants-13-02544-t007" data-position="float">
  <div class="html-table_wrap_td">
    <div class="html-tablepopup html-tablepopup-link" href="#table_body_display_plants-13-02544-t007">
        <img src="https://pub.mdpi-res.com/img/table.png" alt="" role="presentation" width="138" height="79" loading="lazy">
       
    </div>

  </div>
  <div class="html-table_wrap_discription">
    <b>Table 7.</b>
    The different media compositions used in the multiplication trials.
  </div>
</div><div class="html-p">A preliminary multiplication media trial (MT0) was conducted to investigate the impact of plant growth regulators (PGRs) on the occurrence of vitrification. Three variations of MS media (MM2, MM8, and MM9) with and without 0.5 mg/L meta-topolin, the most widely used PGR for cannabis micropropagation [<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 11" reference-link="#B11-plants-13-02544">11</button>,<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 30" reference-link="#B30-plants-13-02544">30</button>,<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 31" reference-link="#B31-plants-13-02544">31</button>,<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 61" reference-link="#B61-plants-13-02544">61</button>,<button tabindex="0" class="html-reference" aria-label="Reference 62" reference-link="#B62-plants-13-02544">62</button>], were tried (<a href="#plants-13-02544-t007" class="html-table">Table 7</a>) on the different stage II explants as described in <a href="#sec4dot2dot3-plants-13-02544" class="html-sec">Section 4.2.3</a> using media sterilization as described in <a href="#sec4dot3dot1-plants-13-02544" class="html-sec">Section 4.3.1</a>. Each media treatment consisted of 15 units (5 explants per container) and was repeated thrice (N = 45). The vitrification data were collected after 4 weeks of culture. Vitrification was scored on a scale of 5 to 1, from highly vitrified (5) to no vitrification (1) (<a href="#plants-13-02544-f005" class="html-fig">Figure 5</a>).</div><div class="html-p">Multiplication media trial 1 (MT1) was conducted to assess the influence of stage II explant type and media composition on height. Five distinct types of stage II explants, as described in <a href="#sec4dot3dot1-plants-13-02544" class="html-sec">Section 4.3.1</a>, were cultured on eleven different media (T1–T11) (<a href="#plants-13-02544-t007" class="html-table">Table 7</a>). Explant heights were recorded (<a href="#plants-13-02544-f0A2" class="html-fig">Figure A2</a>a). Replication, media sterilization, culture maintenance, and data collection time were the same as for MT0.</div><div class="html-p">The aim of multiplication media trial 2 (MT2) was to further optimize the best-performing media for the best-performing explant types as established by MT1. The E1 and E2 stage II explants were tested on modifications of MM8 and MM9 media compositions. Agar was increased from 7 gm/L to 9.5 gm/L, and the calcium gluconate and calcium nitrate compositions were modified to assess the effect of additional calcium and nitrogen in the media (<a href="#plants-13-02544-t007" class="html-table">Table 7</a>). Explant height and vitrification were recorded the same as for MT1 and MT0, respectively. Rooting and health were assessed on a 5–1 score (<a href="#plants-13-02544-f0A2" class="html-fig">Figure A2</a>c,d)m and multiplication rate was recorded (number of explants from a single explant for the next subculture). Replication, media sterilization, culture maintenance, and data collection time were the same as for MT0.</div></section><section id="sec4dot3dot3-plants-13-02544" type=""><h4 class="" data-nested="3">  4.3.3. Indexing the Media Based on MT2 Results</h4><div class="html-p">The media compositions were ranked according to the index:<div class="html-disp-formula-info" id="">
      <div class="f">
        Index = Height + Multiplication Rate+ Health + Rooting − Vitrification
      </div>
      <div class="l">
      
      </div>
    </div>
          Height, multiplication rate, health, and rooting were considered to be positively correlated to TC success and were scored as follows: a = 5, b = 4, c = 3, d = 2, e–g = 1. Vitrification was considered detrimental to TC success and score as: a = 1, b = 2, c = 3, d = 4, e–g = 5.</div></section></section><section id="sec4dot4-plants-13-02544" type=""><h3 class="html-italic" data-nested="2">  4.4. Acclimatization</h3><div class="html-p">Stage II explants started rooting after 2–4 weeks when transferred in fresh hormone free multiplication media. Well-rooted plantlets (6–8 cm) were deflasked (taken out from the medium) after 4 weeks in multiplication media. The roots of the plantlet were treated with an antifungal (Pervicure fungicide from Crop Science Australia) solution, and excess agar was removed. After that, plantlets were transplanted onto coco peat plugs (Eazy Plug Coco Peat Propagation Tray from Hydrocentre Hydroponics, Australia) and kept covered in a mini greenhouse with movable air lid boxes. For the first weeks, the plantlets were sprayed with water every day to keep the humidity level high. After 10 days, slowly the movable air lid was adjusted to allow the humidity to reduce, followed by no cover, and maintained in a greenhouse. Plants were then transplanted in small plastic pots and finally in bigger pots. One month after deflasking, the survival frequency of regenerated plants was recorded.</div></section><section id="sec4dot5-plants-13-02544" type=""><h3 class="html-italic" data-nested="2">  4.5. Compilation of the Optimized Protocol and Testing on Different Cannabis Varieties</h3><div class="html-p">The optimized protocol was tested on eight genetically diverse varieties, including both <span class="html-italic">Cannabis</span> (&lt;1% THC) and hemp (&gt;1% THC) (<a href="#plants-13-02544-t005" class="html-table">Table 5</a>). Sixty explants were collected from 3-week-old plants. The stage 1 (microshoot tip) explants were surface sterilized using 1% NaOCl for 30 min from <a href="#sec4dot2dot1-plants-13-02544" class="html-sec">Section 4.2.1</a> and initiated in the initiation medium (IM3) from <a href="#sec4dot3dot1-plants-13-02544" class="html-sec">Section 4.3.1</a>. After the 2–3 weeks post-initiation, the healthy, contamination-free stage II explants were subcultured in MM12 media without hormone from <a href="#sec4dot3dot2-plants-13-02544" class="html-sec">Section 4.3.2</a>. The explants were consistently maintained in the same media across three subcultures, each separated by a four-week interval, through the process of transferring them into fresh media for each subculture stage. The rooted plantlets were deflasked four weeks after the last subculture and transferred into coco peat plug and maintained in a mini greenhouse for two weeks for acclimatization. The fully rooted plants were transplanted in a bigger pot before transferring them to a greenhouse.</div></section><section id="sec4dot6-plants-13-02544" type=""><h3 class="html-italic" data-nested="2">  4.6. Statistical Analysis</h3><div class="html-p">Data from all experiments were analyzed using R 4.1.0 (R-studio 23.03.0 build 386 © 2009–2023 Posit Software, PBC) and the packages (ggplot2, ggpubr, tidyverse, broom, agricolae, reshape2, emmeans, dplyr, dunn.test, corrplot, and Hmisc). All experimental data were collected at the individual explant level, which served as the units of replication in the study. For the initiation stage (stage I), where data were collected as a binary response (yes/no), data were analyzed using a generalized linear model with a binomial distribution with main effects compared using Tukey’s HSD at <span class="html-italic">p</span> &lt; 0.05 for mean separation. For multiplication stage (stage II), in MT1, the height data were log transformed to achieve near-normal distribution based on Shapiro–Wilk Test and Q–Q (quantile–quantile) plot. A two-way ANOVA was performed with explant type and treatment specified as factors. The treatments were compared using predicted values to select the best-performing media. Then, the data for the best-performing media were pooled, and a one-way ANOVA was performed to establish the effect of explant type for these media. For MT2, non-transformed data of traits (height, health, vitrification, rooting, and multiplication rate) were evaluated using normality tests Shapiro–Wilk and Q–Q (quantile-quantile) plot, which revealed that traits including height did not follow a normal distribution. This necessitated using the Kruskal–Wallis test, succeeded by Dunn’s test for conducting multiple comparisons for MT2. The parameters were compared using a correlation dot plot.</div></section></section></div><div id="Author_Contributions" data-heading-anchor="1"><section class="html-notes"><h2>Author Contributions</h2><div class="html-p">Conceptualization, R.D., T.K. and J.C.M.; methodology, R.D.; formal analysis, R.D.; investigation, R.D.; resources, J.C.M.; data curation, R.D.; writing—original draft preparation, R.D.; writing—review and editing, J.C.M. and T.K.; visualization, R.D.; supervision, T.K. and J.C.M.; project administration, T.K.; funding acquisition, T.K. All authors have read and agreed to the published version of the manuscript.</div></section></div><div id="Funding" data-heading-anchor="1"><section class="html-notes"><h2>Funding</h2><div class="html-p">This research was co-funded by Cymra Life Sciences and Southern Cross University.</div></section></div><div id="Data_Availability_Statement" data-heading-anchor="1"><section class="html-notes"><h2>Data Availability Statement</h2><div class="html-p">Data are contained within the article.</div></section></div><div id="Acknowledgments" data-heading-anchor="1"><section id="html-ack" class="html-ack"><h2>Acknowledgments</h2><div class="html-p">The authors thank Simon Pettinger and Mathew Turner for excellent discussions and stimulating ideas. The authors acknowledge Professor Bronwyn Barkla for allowing experimental Cannabis work for this study under the authority issued by the New South Wales Ministry of Health, Australia.</div></section></div><div id="Conflicts_of_Interest" data-heading-anchor="1"><section class="html-notes"><h2>Conflicts of Interest</h2><div class="html-p">The authors declare that this study received funding from Cymra Life Sciences. The funder was not involved in the study design, collection, analysis, interpretation of data, the writing of this article or the decision to submit it for publication.</div></section></div><section><div id="Appendix_A" data-heading-anchor="1"><section id="app1-plants-13-02544" type=""><h2 data-nested="1">  Appendix A</h2><div class="html-fig-wrap" id="plants-13-02544-f0A1" data-appendix="true" data-position="anchor">

      Effect of different media modifications and explant types on height (N = 15 × 3). (<b>a</b>) Height variation for predicted mean of all treatments without explant-type effect. (<b>b</b>) Effect of explant type (E1—primary shoot tips usually have many nodes with shorter internodal segments, E2—secondary shoot tips usually have fewer nodes with long internodal segments, E3—secondary shoot tip with primary node, E4—small secondary shoot tip with primary node, and E5—primary nodes with or without emerging bud) on height in MM8 and MM9 media in Media Trial 1. The means were compared using Tukey’s honest significant difference with <span class="html-italic">p</span> &lt; 0.05, significant differences are indicated by different letters for each treatment.

      Click here to enlarge figure
    </a></p> -->

data-testid="keyboard-backspace" style="" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 16 16"></svg> Previous</div></a><a href="/2223-7747/13/18/2545" class="flex flex-1 flex-col justify-between gap-md <script nonce="wuyl3hu1XYztKJzvhczgWrVU" type="application/json" data-nuxt-data="nuxt-app" data-ssr="true" id="__NUXT_DATA__">[["ShallowReactive",1],{"data":2,"state":621,"once":988,"_errors":989,"serverRendered":562,"path":991},["ShallowReactive",3],{"strapi-config":4,"2223-7747-13-18-2544":6,"article-2223-7747-13-18-2544":528},{"cacheTime":5},1,{"contents":7,"tableOfContents":8,"references":41,"abstract":521,"keywords":522},"\u003Chtml>\u003Chead>\u003C/head>\u003Cbody>\u003Cdiv id=\"Abstract\" data-heading-anchor=\"1\">\u003Csection class=\"html-abstract\" id=\"html-abstract\">\n            \u003Ch2 id=\"html-abstract-title\">Abstract\u003C/h2>\n            \u003Cdiv class=\"html-p\">Producing uniform \u003Cspan class=\"html-italic\">Cannabis sativa (Cannabis)\u003C/span> for medicinal/recreational flower production through sexual propagation has been problematic, leading to dominance of clonal propagation from “mother plants” in the cannabinoid industry, which also faces significant limitations. \u003Cspan class=\"html-italic\">Cannabis\u003C/span> tissue culture (TC) methods have been developed to overcome these challenges, but the long-term health and maintenance of \u003Cspan class=\"html-italic\">Cannabis\u003C/span> explants in TC have been largely overlooked in previous studies. The current study focused on the development of an efficient and optimized micropropagation protocol covering the entire process, with a specific focus on the health and performance in the multiplication stage. Multiplication media were formulated hormone-free to avoid longer-term vitrification issues, resulting in single-main-shoot cultures rather than multiple-shoot cultures. This instigated the use of stage II explant types different from the standard shoot tips previously used for multiple shoot cultures. Multiplication media were further improved from the basal salt composition via nitrogen and calcium additives. The optimized protocol was used on eight diverse \u003Cspan class=\"html-italic\">Cannabis\u003C/span> cultivars to test its applicability across various genetic backgrounds. Results indicated that the protocol was effective for conservation purposes across all cultivars and achieved good long-term multiplication rates for some but not all. The outcomes of this study mark a significant stride towards an efficient \u003Cspan class=\"html-italic\">Cannabis\u003C/span> TC methodology ready for more comprehensive industrial applications.\u003C/div>\n    \u003C/section>\u003C/div>\u003Cdiv id=\"html-keywords\">\n        \u003Cdiv class=\"html-gwd-group\">\n                            \u003Cdiv id=\"html-keywords-title\">Keywords:\u003C/div>\n                                                                    \u003Ca href=\"/search?q=Cannabis+tissue+culture\" class=\"html-keywords-link\">\u003Ci>Cannabis\u003C/i> tissue culture\u003C/a>;                    \u003Ca href=\"/search?q=Cannabis+sativa\" class=\"html-keywords-link\">\u003Ci>Cannabis sativa\u003C/i>\u003C/a>;                    \u003Ca href=\"/search?q=micropropagation\" class=\"html-keywords-link\">micropropagation\u003C/a>;                    \u003Ca href=\"/search?q=shoot+regeneration\" class=\"html-keywords-link\">shoot regeneration\u003C/a>;                    \u003Ca href=\"/search?q=vitrification\" class=\"html-keywords-link\">vitrification\u003C/a>        \u003C/div>\n\n            \u003C/div>\u003Cdiv id=\"html-graphical\">\n    \u003Cdiv class=\"html-gwd-group\">\u003C/div>\n\u003C/div>\u003Cdiv id=\"Introduction\" data-heading-anchor=\"1\">\u003Csection id=\"sec1-plants-13-02544\" type=\"intro\">\u003Ch2 data-nested=\"1\">  1. Introduction\u003C/h2>\u003Cdiv class=\"html-p\">Global \u003Cspan class=\"html-italic\">Cannabis sativa\u003C/span> (\u003Cspan class=\"html-italic\">Cannabis\u003C/span>) markets have grown and matured in recent years as an increasing number of countries have legalized \u003Cspan class=\"html-italic\">Cannabis\u003C/span> for medicinal and/or recreational purposes as well as industrial uses. For instance, \u003Cspan class=\"html-italic\">Cannabis\u003C/span> has been legalized for medicinal purposes in various U.S. states, and CBD from industrial hemp is legal country-wide since the 2018 Farm Bill, while Canada and Thailand legalized it for both medicinal and recreational uses in 2018 and 2023, respectively [\u003Cbutton tabindex=\"0\" class=\"html-reference\" aria-label=\"Reference 1\" reference-link=\"#B1-plants-13-02544\">1\u003C/button>,\u003Cbutton tabindex=\"0\" class=\"html-reference\" aria-label=\"Reference 2\" reference-link=\"#B2-plants-13-02544\">2\u003C/button>,\u003Cbutton tabindex=\"0\" class=\"html-reference\" aria-label=\"Reference 3\" reference-link=\"#B3-plants-13-02544\">3\u003C/button>,\u003Cbutton tabindex=\"0\" class=\"html-reference\" aria-label=\"Reference 4\" reference-link=\"#B4-plants-13-02544\">4\u003C/button>]. Similarly, Germany ended \u003Cspan class=\"html-italic\">Cannabis\u003C/span> prohibition in 2021, becoming the second G7 nation to do so [\u003Cbutton tabindex=\"0\" class=\"html-reference\" aria-label=\"Reference 5\" reference-link=\"#B5-plants-13-02544\">5\u003C/button>] after Canada, and since April 2024, a legal framework for recreational use has been implemented.
